RATING: 5
5 plays / 4,5,6 players (1st edition & 2nd edition)

LIKE: Hard decisions to use which cards where. Cool theme, somewhat quick game. The card mutations are a great mechanism.

DISLIKE: The game is based heavily on luck of the draw, and a couple card combos seem very powerful. The late game is terrible: leaders will snowball and those lagging behind get trounced. Carnivores are more difficult than herbivores to maintain. Herbivores can easily circumvent them. 

OVERALL: Early game is fun, but fall behind and there's little chance for catching up. In one game, my carnivore ate the weakest player, but couldn't catch the strongest player. It's the late game that drags. Downrated after several plays.

RATING: 5
The best part of this game is that it plays quite fast and has exactly the right length. BUT, we achieved this only when we chose to perform our actions simultaneously. I reckon that with too serious and tight players the game will be too slow for what it is.

RATING: 6
1st play: An interesting game, that is easy to introduce to non-gamers. There is some strategy to picking the right species and trying to make them thrive. I found myself wanting to make a carnivore all game and didn't really draw the card to do it. Most of the traits, it doesn't matter as much if you can't draw, but carnivore is such an important trait that I would like it better if I could freely choose carnivore or herbivore. The art is great and the theme makes sense though.

2nd play: I had no trouble getting Carnivore cards this time, and the game was more interesting for it. Overcoming other species' defenses can be a challenge, making carnivore a hard but rewarding creature type to play. There's more strategy than I initially gave it credit for.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.75
What an absolute delight of a game! Very, very beautiful art! Due to the ever changing variety of species which you'll create in each game, it seems there is no end of replayability. A tactical game, primarily; but it is the queen of tactical games! One must know when is the time to change course and go the way of the carnivore or something, or risk going the way of the dodo.

Cut-throat? Well, I suppose...sorta: I'm not a fan of cutthroat games, but Evolution doesn't bother me at all. Being eaten by a carnivore doesn't really make you curse the opponent's beast, but rather ask yourself, "Now why did I waste my time opting for Foraging when I could easily have had a bright, shiny new defensive shell?" You roll with the punches and it doesn't really seem all that cutthroat...to me, anyways.

A funny thing about Evolution is that it doesn't feel all that...evolution-y! It's more of a game about creating and taking part in a food chain rather than anything dealing with the theory of evolution. Once in a blue moon, you might change a trait; but that sure doesn't feel like your creature's evolved! It feels like your creature has adapted: adapted in an admittedly dramatic way. You are not developing from amoeba to dinosaur to mammal or anything like that: the vast majority of the trait cards show creatures that are fairly contemporary with each other. I don't know if this was the designer's deliberate intention, but for a game with the title "Evolution", it really seems like this could be played with good and friendly humour at your next mixed gathering of evolutionists and creationists! Apart from the title, there's no controversy in this game at all.

RATING: 7.8
A nice and easy to play card based game that captures the theme of evolution very well indeed.

Players take turns to evolve their already existing species with new traits such as long neck or horned, or they can make a whole new species to evolve in future turns. After all players have had a turn evolving or creating their creatures, everybody then feeds the animals from a limited pool of food.

How you evolve your creatures will dictate whether they thrive and continue to grow or die out and become extinct.

There is a good variety of strategies that one can employ  to make their creatures thrive yet the simplicity of the game mechanics can trick you into underestimating the strategy levels involved in a good game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Great game with many interesting choices and no set dominant strategy. You decide how to evolve your species in order to either get food more efficiently or for protection. Those who can adapt to the changing food and carnivore environment are best suited for victory. There are many interesting combinations that can work together to create a powerful species. 

It took me several plays to appreciate how well balanced this game is. At first certain strategies seemed dominant, then after more plays other strategies seemed dominant. As I understood the game better I realized that nearly every combination has a good counter play against it, you just need to "evolve" your strategy during the game. Discovering the various combinations and counter combinations is an "Evolutionary" process in and of itself.

The production copy has great components and slightly modified traits to make the game even better than the playtest version I had played before.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Evolution is a card game simulating the struggle among different species of animals to survive and evolve, competing for food.
The mechanics are very simple and straightforward: in turn, each player plays cards as traits to evolve existing species, or discards cards to raise species' size or population, or create new species.
The flavor emerges both from the interaction among players, and from the different traits cards.
The production value is quite good, with a big prop for the amazing artwork.
The game is enticing, and I am eager to play again!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Initial Impression: Theme and mechanisms are tightly interwoven in this game. Because most of the rules can be understood based on common sense, I can teach this game in just a few short minutes. This highly tactical game seems to have many different paths to victory. Expect heavy amounts of player interaction, both indirect (herbivores scrambling for limited food supplies) and direct (carnivores eating other species). Interesting card combos add flavor to the game. My family took to this game instantly.

RATING: 2
No actual decision making to be made n this in this game, I'm not sure it even qualifies as a game since it basically plays itself. Oh they have climbing and the only way I can hunt them is with climbing? Guess I'll get climbing. Oh they Got defensive herding guess I'll get pack hunting. Its impossible to actually "play" a carnivore as your decisions are always made for you. Often there will be few maybe even only 1 species you're eligible to even try and hunt. Yet the carnivore is a necessary evil to prevent the game form being multiplayer solitaire, which I guess there's nothing wrong with liking. It's just that there are hundreds of games that do that better.

Je beheert kuddes van dieren, die je kan groter maken (in aantal of size).  Via eigenschappen maak je er sterkere dieren van, of hebben ze extra features of worden het carnivoren.

Elke kudde dient gevoed of sterft uit.  Ngl. de soort is dit met planten vanuit de poel, of met vlees door andere dieren te doden.

Gelukselement = welke kaarten (features) krijg je ter beschikking
Maar door slim te combineren is elke combi van kaarten goed bruikbaar.

4 diersoorten houden leek wat veel in een spel met 5 spelers.

Score 7 na een eerste keer spelen.  
Ik had het moeilijk om het overzicht te behouden.
Mogelijks verhoogt de score na een tweede keer spelen.

RATING: 8.5
They finally made a FUN educational game. Cards are extremely thematic, and provide gameplay that is simple to explain, but contains true strategic depth. You are thinking non stop, and playing close attention to the traits your opponent(s) play. What's even cooler is that species actually evolve. If a players animals are repeatedly attacked by their opponents carnivore, they will start playing defensive traits. Cool, thematic implementation of a concept that has so much potential to be boring. Definitely at least a must-play.

RATING: 3
A small beef with this game.  I thought North Star Games would be the one that could make a gamer's game that would be accessible.  Nothing could be further from the truth on this one.  

So my first turn out, I was, "What the hell, I'll make a carnivore."   Everyone else's first turn out was, "What the hell, I'll be sure to have climbing, burrowing, warning call (and negate that carnivore)."   The thing is, they couldn't have known as I was casting traits secretly---so this was just knowledge that everyone 'just knew' except me, the new guy at the table.

Right out the gate, there was nothing I could eat, and he starves to death.   This would be fine, but all the cards I laid-out thinking were good moves, were gone.  I was down a creature, and I got less cards the next round.  Being down early, you're hosed.   

I'm fine coming in last in my first game.  I'm fine knowing there's not a chance in hell of winning.  I'm not fine being a position where I wasn't concerned about making an impact or doing well, but rather having something meaningful to do, at all.

Nothing I've ever played captures the essence of making me feel fucking stupid like this one did.  The people I was playing with were being ultra nice to me both out of courtesy and also clearly I wasn't the guy they needed to worry about.

While the mechanics were all there, and the mechanics of the game were sound, I did NOT enjoy this, and I can't think of anyone I would introduce this to.  I like my games to at least give a newbie a chance or create a safe space to have shit to do.  Someone said, "If you win your first game of this, someone is being very nice to you."   

Basically though, fuck this game.

I rated this a '3' because I would give it a shot again if I were asked, I would likely know better than to make these mistakes...but it's a poor game that treats new players this badly.

RATING: 9.5
This game achieves unbelievable depth and variety of strategy despite a very simple ruleset; this is the trademark of a timeless classic.  The only hesitation I have (which prevents me from rating this a ten) is that it feels at times that things like flying creatures are conspicuously missing, to the point where it feels like some potential traits were withheld in order to make an expansion more appealing.

This isn't uncommon, of course, but it makes it so that this game comes across as almost perfect, with a couple gaps, instead of utterly perfect.

Initial Impressions:
------------------------
Actually thought I would like this one WAY more than i did.  By only getting 4 cards, and not doing any sort of draft, the game felt like it kind of played its self.  It wasn't horrible long, but if you get behind early, it seems almost impossible to catch up (at least in a 6p game).  Something I'd play again if someone really wanted to, but not something I'll be requesting anytime soon...

Update:
------------------------
Found it more interesting with less players, but still I don't find the card combos that interesting.  I also feel like without card drafting, your control is much smaller, since you have no idea what your opponents will draw.  Still not sure I get the hype on this one.

RATING: 7
Played at Spiel '15.
Reimplementation of Evolution: The Origin of Species. The possibilities for long-term strategy are much improved. However, the amount of luck is quite similar and so is the overall feel of the game. 
The artwork is the aspect of the game that changed the most. The new game has very colorful, almost expressionistic images, whereas the images in the old game are simple but stylish.
Looking at the price and the box size of the new game, the old one deserves an equal recommendation.
The biggest improvement compared to Evolution: The Origion of Species is the player influence on the amount of food that is available.
The biggest downgrade in my opinion is the only-three-traits-per-species rule, making Evolution a much more static game than its predecessor (which, granted, is a bit too dynamic).

RATING: 8
I really like this game. It has a sufficient level of complexity and variations in style of play, yet is not as much up to chance as other games of it's type. The mechanics are a bit difficult to understand at first, and I've played a few times in which new players don't realize their mistakes until many turns later. The whole field has to balance the number of herbivores versus carnivores in order for all to profit. 

Downside: there are clearly a few winning strategies that are dominant. In my experience, the player who is best able to use the Cooperate ability will win the game. This is a highly broken ability that has the ability to stack on top of itself and create chain-feeding reactions.

RATING: 9
Is Evolution good? : Yes

That said, it seems to get better and better after each game. There are, as everyone says, "many paths to victory", and a game winning strategy can be poor the next game.
This very simple game can become surprisingly "violent".

The theme is very present as we literally feel the struggle for life. This feeling may only come after a few games, but it's very present.

A unique game, that's what makes it great! And the design is beautiful!

Can't wait to try Flight Expansion!

RATING: 8
Love the curve on this game - just when you think things are nice and safe it becomes a deadly fight for survival. Things really can turn on... well, a turn. Even with 2, this game can get desperate fast!

This is a really nice little combo builder. It's accessible but rife with tough decisions, and extremely interactive. The pacing and playtime just feel perfect for its weight. There random element does more to present you with opportunities, rather than destroy your strategies.

Speaking of that, this game does something pretty interesting for a combo-builder: rather than rely on tons of card synergies for its strategies, this game has comparatively few. There really aren't many unique cards, so the exact builds of your species won't change too much from game to game - instead this game is more about your [i]general[/i] strategy. And since so much is about managing your card economy and timing, you have good reason to do things differently from game to game without having to memorize a ton of content. The result in a game that is more [i]instantly[/i] fun and understandable than just about anything else in the genre.

I also usually dislike "take that!" mechanics but they are extremely well-integrated here. A lot has been done to make losing species feel like a natural part of the game, rather than a kick in the pants.

While 2p is fun, this is better with more. With two, it's easy for someone to get a species advantage early and then the other player is stuck playing catch-up for the rest of the game. It's still tense and interesting, but not ideal.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Played at The Secret Cabal's Beermongers Christmas event 2014.  Obtained at GenCon 2016.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I bought evolution on a whim, having fairly low expectations for it.  I thought it could end up being a little "too" simple.  Even the first playtest didn't blow me away.  I was afraid that I would master the game too quickly or become bored with the limited number of traits available.

But something kept happening whenever I let my friends choose which games they wanted to play.  Evolution looked like the "cool" one that they really wanted to try.  

To me the game feels a bit like poker, where you're trying to construct the best group of species with your hand to get the most chips (food) in the pot (watering hole)

Pros:
1) It's very simple to set up and clean up
2) Easy to learn, hard to master
3) lots and lots of replayability
4) emergent and synergistic properties between the card types amount to over 4,000 different types of species, giving players a huge amount of strategic freedom and multiple ways to win
5) the game can accommodate 2-6 (but really shines with 3 to 4 players) 
6) The game has an ebb and flow of advantages, so that you really have to plan ahead and think about present and future turns
7) this is hardly solitaire, players have to be keenly aware of what their opponents are up to and outplay them
8) Forcing an opponent to eat or kill off their own species is immensely satisfying


Cons
1) The provided cheat sheets are helpful, but don't entirely explain some of the key points in the game, namely carnivore feeding.
2) The game can be difficult if you have a few badly constructed or unlucky turns.  If your opponent(s) end up with a significantly more species than you, they'll draw more trait cards and will be more likely to win.  
3) There is no such thing as a super-carnivore, but it definitely feels like some herbivore arrangements are impenetrable to attack and very powerful in the long run.

Overall, winning depends on a fair amount of luck but also a good application of skill to play the cards life dealt you.  9/10 would play it any chance I get

RATING: 9
This game hits the spot of somehow being very simple, but at the same time being deeply strategic and tactical! It seems so simple at first glance, but this game is a flower that opens and blooms the more you explore the synergies between the different traits.  There are a lot of interesting decisions to be made from turn 1 of this game all through the end.  This game is more than a game with the them of evolution, rather it is a game about playing the people and the table where you are evolving your play throughout the game. Sometimes you are evolving preemptively, but more often reactivity to the evolution and introduction of new species by each player's ecosystem(not to mention your own- the idea of introducing a new species in your own tableau just so you can feed off of it is mind-boggling and exciting!). Evolution is not just the name of the game, it's the heart and soul of the game! Such an underrated game that has so much to offer than just a fun time (although it has that in spades)!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.25
Evolution is a good next step for the North Star line - a game that's still pretty easy to learn, but there is more strategic depth here. The components are nicely done - great art.

This game is possibly most similar to Small World, in that each round players are trying to decide if they should expand by adding more species and enhancing them with traits, or acknowledging that some of them might die off. Since all that matters is how much food they consume along the way, it's perfectly fine to just let those species go sometimes.

While I like this game, right now I feel like it's ripe for expansions to make it a really satisfying experience for regular gamers. In particular it's overly challenging to have a carnivorous species, which I think could be easily corrected with more trait cards added to help them.

RATING: 7.6
I was absolutely smitten with this game when I first got it and have thoroughly enjoyed the 4 plays I've gotten out of it so far.  The theme jumps right out at you and forces you into a lush prehistoric world before humans decided to make the Earth their personal dumpster, where animals are evolving to better compete for survival.  It really feels like you're evolving through the ages to stay relevant as a species and the way you evolve depends on how much food is available and what other species are co-existing with you.

RATING: N/A
[2017-04-14 - First Impression (Note: I'm talking about the german version)]
I've enjoyed my first play of Evolution. There's quite a bit of hand management, some nice player interaction and also room for clever plays. It was fun trying to search for the best combination of traits within the current environment. I wouldn't mind playing this game more often, although I'm not sure if this will happen. 

The problem seems to be me and my gaming group. Most of them have a background playing Magic: The Gathering which means they want to understand the interdependencies of the traits in detail and therefore prefer perfectly defined keywords. At least the german version lacks in this department: Some cards talk about "eating food", some cards talk about "taking food". We figured this would be the same, but why are two different terms used for one thing? This "legal" questions slowed the game really down and left some of us with a slightly negative first impression.

Maybe I'll just wait for the app and play it a bunch of times so that I know the official rulings of most of the edge cases. However, I'd like to explore this game a little more.

RATING: 6
My rating and comments are my reflections on the two player game.  I never played it with more, and I could see it being a really fun game with four or more.

The theme really shines in this game.  It's all about trying to get a big share of food and shafting everyone else.  It's a battle for watering hole dominance, and not everyone is going to come out alive. 

Attacking other players can be really in your face, and I don't like the wild swing between defensive and offensive play.

If you can't handle getting your stuff destroyed, or destroying other players then you may not like this.  You can really mess someone's day up and vice versa.

I think everything about this game design is brilliant, but the fun I have with it is very limited. I'm not really into games where every turn is an attempt to one-up everyone else, and Evolution feels that way to me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.75
This is a great card-based game in which players use different traits to build their species in a competition for food with the other players' species.  The game is highly thematic with animal traits such as "long neck" and "foraging" that gain you the edge in food consumption.  Of course, players have the option of evolving their species into a carnivore to prey on the other animals.  Traits such as "intelligence" and "pack hunting" will aid in your hunting endeavors while other players use defensive traits such as "hard shell" or "horns" to counter carnivores.  The way each species evolves to deal with predators/prey or food shortages is extremely thematic.  One drawback is the luck of the card draw.  However, I still enjoyed this one!  Recommended.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
In Evolution, players adapt their species in a dynamic ecosystem where food is scarce and predators lurk. Traits like Hard Shell and Horns will protect your species from Carnivores, while a Long Neck will help them get food that others cannot reach. With over 4,000 ways to evolve your species, every game becomes a different adventure.

Evolution packs a surprising amount of variety for a game with simple rules. The variety comes from the synergies between the trait cards and from the different personalities at the table. Some players thrive on creating Carnivores to wreak havoc on their fellow players. Others prefer to stay protected and mind their own business. Evolution encourages both play styles by giving each of them multiple paths to victory. And it is the mix of play styles at the table that ultimately determines the ecosystem in which the players are adapting. So gather your friends and see who can best adapt to the changing world around them.

"The theme of evolution is not just tacked on: it drives play." - Nature (the world's most prestigious scientific journal)
Evolution was covered in the journal Nature. The article was written by Stuart West, a Professor of Evolutionary Biology at the University of Oxford who has used Evolution in his undergraduate class. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.25

RATING: 7
Tierfütterung in Darwinland: mit Hilfe von Eigenschaftskarten macht man vorsintflutliche Tierarten fit für den Überlebenskampf am Futterteich. Denn jeder Fress-Chip ist ein Siegpunkt. Zu Beginn noch ein harmloses Gerangel von Pflanzenfressern, mutiert das Kartenspielchen mit dem Einsatz von Fleischfressern zu einem Kampf gegen Mitspieler. Die Fleischfresser sind das Salz in der Suppe, die ohne diese kompetitive Note in einer müder Verteilung von Siegpunktplättchen verenden würde. Andererseits kann man den Fleischfresser mit entsprechendem Schutz am langen Arm auch ziemlich verhungern lassen. So bleibt ein lineares Spielpürree aus Kenner-Enginebau, Taktikspiel und Beutelei im schönen Retro-Quartettkarten-Look.

RATING: 6
There is a large amount of guesswork and second guesswork at what other players are trying to accomplish, making for an interesting table. Carnivores offer a method of directly attacking other players, but the attacks are by and large fairly inoffensive. It is incredibly difficult to maintain a carnivore with the number of defensive opportunities available most turns.
It plays partially like an engine-building game, but due to how volatile those engines are, it ultimately comes out more like point-salad style optimization played through the meta-game based upon how much food other players populate into the watering hole.

RATING: 5.21
I had high expectations for this card game, because (as a Dominant Species fan) I love its theme.

It is a very clunky game, with run away leader issues: once one player gets to become a carnivore, unless you have a lot of defensive modifiers, consider yourself out of the game.

I hate when someone convince the group to play a "card" game for being "shorter", and it tooks longer than a "board" one would really take. The argument "let's play a card game because it is shorter" is a big fallacy, and this game is one example that takes too long for what it is.

The game's owner also commented he removed some cards from KS campaign, because they are unbalanced. I just thought, without saying, "...even more?". 

I would not play it again unless there wasn't other (better) options.

It's a real snoozefest. I'm glad I had a chance to try before buying.

RATING: 6
I often love combo card games like this. And the theme is very much up my alley! Evolution has some good things going for it: beautiful artwork, simple ruleset, and opportunities to try different combinations of cards for maximum impact. However, by the end of the game I looked back on the experience and didn't quite recall myself having much fun or excitement. It was novel to explore its possibilities. The cards all worked and nothing was wonky. But I felt the game made it too easy to take on a position of either defensive turtling or downright bash the supposed leader.  With just over 15 card types (which might seem like a lot) I just didn't feel future games of this would feel varied enough to hold my interest. The need to respond to opponent card play felt rather scripted, with defense cards specifically naming the attack cards that they would block (which I get is what evolution is all about but it's too simplified here). Also, didn't like how everything looked on the table - sort of a sprawling mess of cards and boards. I think it's well designed and perhaps the expansion adds just enough variety to it that I would enjoy the experience more; but when I consider the games out there I love have similar mechanics, Evolution just didn't get on the list.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game's representation of darwinian adaptation is simply brilliant. 

The rules are simple and unambiguous. They establish a clear, basic framework of a limited food supply and player options for changing that supply or their relationship to it by mutating their species traits, or changing one of two core aspects, population and body size, that affect their consumption of food and resistance to predators (respectively). All of these options are allowed through the multiple-use cards in their hand, giving players a satisfying set of decisions every turn about how to best use this hand to adapt to the constantly shifting game state (e.g. inject more/less food into the supply? change to a carnivore to start attacking other players? add traits to prevent them from attacking us? increase our population to eat more and get more points? etc. etc. etc.)

That's the entire game, in terms of mechanics, but the emergent butterfly-effects that unfurl during gameplay are spectacular.

It's a sign of excellent game design when a game's complexity manifests in its play and dynamic interactions between players, rather than overly legislative rule sets or overloaded point options. 

I also enjoy the creative aspect of imagining my own species based on the weird trait combinations that I've assigned. It has a similar appeal to monster creation in Shadows of Malice. An armoured herd of giraffe-like long-necked creatures with a maxed out, mastodon body size? Sure, I can imagine that!

RATING: 8
What a great game. The components in this are very well done. Beautiful artwork on the cards and even the little players bags were a nice touch. The food bank board is sturdy and beautiful as well.

The game itself is very easy to learn and teach in a short period of time. It's a great balance between the herbivores and the carnivores and the game doesn't become overly cutthroat, as your strategies will constantly be switching during the game. It all really works well and is loads of fun on top of it.

Do be sure you get the correct 2nd edition of the game if you're planning on picking up the Flight expansion, as there are copys out there that have the wrong decks and you'll need to buy a new deck from the company if you want all the cards to match. As far as I know, this is only a issue with the games being sold at Barnes & Noble and Books a Million, but it's best to be sure. The edition you want has a yellow fertile card on the back of the box, rather than a green one.

I'd say this is a good game for everyone, from gamers to family and friends. Check out the videos and judge for yourself. I'm glad I did and can't wait to try this with the expansion.

RATING: 6
Early yet so my judgment isn't final.  It's well designed.  The fundamental game flow, a boardwide ecosystem in an evershifting equilibrium, is compelling.

But the game runs slow for such a light game.  I would strongly recommend using the simultaneous rules, even at the expense of a bit of strategy.  My biggest issue, however, is that it's so incremental that it features almost no points of tension.  It's a long-run slog of progressive buildup without any identifiable memorable moments.  You'll win or lose but it's unlikely you'll pinpoint any decisive card or turn.

RATING: 8
After one play, I can definitively say I like it, but then again I like most tactical card games. The theme really shines through on this one. The decision as to when to put certain traits on certain species is a skill that will have to be learned over time. All my critiques of this game will probably go away upon future plays. First, the game is a little long for what it is (players will get more efficient and speed up play over time). Second, it's hard to see what traits are on what species, what they do, and how they interact with each other when you are late in a four player game and all the cards are sprawled out on the table (all of this will become second nature once we know the traits better). Finally, the rules for when to take food onto your species boards versus in your food bag based on special traits are unclear (nothing a BGG search can't fix). I look forward to playing this some more.

RATING: 7
Hosszúnyakú üreglakó páncélos
Lehetőségem volt kipróbálni a játékot, amelyről előtte már rengeteget hallottam, olvastam a különböző fórumokon.
Első hallásra nagyon megtetszett, így érdeklődve vártam mit is tud.
A kis zacskók, kezdőjátékos dinó-jelző (szerintem ezzel mindenki előad egy kis show-t) és kezdő állatfaj kiosztása után gyors szabálymagyarázat (egyszerű szabályok, nopara) jöhet a „kártyázás”. Lehetőségeid szerint több fajt állítasz elő boszorkánykonyhádban azzal a céllal, hogy a legtöbb győzelmi pontnak megfelelő korongot szerezd meg (egyszerűen zabáltatni kell őket és kész – bár nem olyan egyszerű, mint amilyennek hangzik). Aránylag gyors a játékmenet, a kártyákat pár forduló után ismerni fogod, bár a nyeréshez nem árt, ha szerencsés laphúzáson kívül egy-egy jó kombinációt is elő tudsz állítani. Örök dilemmád lesz, hogy ragadozzak vagy békés növényevő legyek? Természetesen húsevőként tudsz gonoszkodni és azokat felenni az elérhető húsikából, akik nem szimpatikusak. Itt azért a királycsinálás általam „utált” verziója is bekövetkezik (javasolt azokat enni, akik valószínűleg vezetnek). A játék során egészen „eszement” fajokat vagy képes megteremteni, de mindent a túlélésért. A húzópakli elfogyásával a játék is véget ér és következik a végelszámolás.
Kellemes játék, de valamiért nem fogott meg. Ez csak szubjektív vélemény, de én inkább játszom mást helyette. Egy próbát mindenképpen megér!

RATING: 9
I am normally not a fan of card games, but the Evolution series does a fantastic job of merging theme with strategy.  A very "thinky" game with simple but high quality components and artwork.  Grow your species in size and population to compete for food resources with other players.  Evolve your species by adding or removing traits to outcompete your opponents, avoid (their) predators, or feed (your) predators.  The Flight and Climate expansions add interesting elements to gameplay.

RATING: 8.3

-Very easy to teach, yet have some depth.
-The elegant mechanics is going super smoothly with the theme.
-Because of the high interaction between the players you must constantly adjust you strategy to your enemies.
-One of the best comboing game out there.
-Outstanding artwork.

-It is overpriced, by not just a rabbit but more likely a giant.
-There aren't enough cards. I wished if there were more. Because of this i don't know if it will stand the test of time or it will be shrink to the bottom of your sheves later on.

RATING: 7.75
Evolution is a very impressive game. Like other NorthStar games, this one has obviously been thoroughly playtested, and the rules are boiled down to what as simple a flow as possible, but this statement belies the depths of the game.

Get cards (multi-use as money and as traits), setup the food pool, play cards, and eat (VP)- but the cards have a lot going on, and there are many cool interactions here to explore.

Evolution does a great job of grabbing the theme and putting into the mix here, because the traits added to animals greatly change how they fare at food and growth, and food = survival (and more VP), so you are drawn into the survival of your creatures.

The simplicity and depth combined is pretty elegant- and the dino art is great, too.

Cannot start this at less than 7.75; this is a terrific game to bring the family to, or a bunch a hungry gamers- I look forward to many more plays.

RATING: 8
(10/18/2014) Comment based on two plays.

North Star Games, which is well known for popular party game titles, such as Say Anything or Wits and Wagers, has produced a family-friendly game that has enough meat to satisfy veteran gamers. This is a quick card-playing, species evolution game, along the lines of Evo, with simple rules, fast play, little downtime, and plenty of interesting choices.

What is different from Evo is that players can spawn a number of new species, and each can be differentiated with up to three traits, as well as population and body size. The play is card driven, where each card represents either a food value OR a specific trait (one of over 20?) OR population growth OR growth in body size. Each specie starts out rather generic, with one population and a size-one body, but they quickly can get unique in terms of traits.

Each turn, players get a minimum of 3x cards, and 1x card for each specie that they have. Then then secretly allocate one card to the center, to determine the amount of plant food available in a communal pool--the food value on the cards are added up, and that many food tokens are placed in the center. At first, this seems rather meaningless early on in the game when everyone has one or two species to feed, but later on, food can become a critical factor when everyone's species have grown in population. High food value cards also tend to have more desirable traits, so players will begin to agonize in the latter half of the game--put out a lot of plant food (that everyone else will share in) or keep and play a desirable trait to give one of your species a significant advantage?

Then in turn order, players play cards:
1) Discard a card to spawn a new specie--a player gets a new specie tableau, on which to track population and body size.
2) Discard a card to grow the population of an existing specie by one.
3) Discard a card to grow the body size of an existing specie by one.
4) Allocate a card (face down) to add a trait to an existing specie (up to a max of three traits--you can add more traits, but must discard to keep it at three).
5) Keep any unused cards, possibly to activate "Intelligence", which is one of the traits.

Once everyone has played their cards, and they can play as many as they wish, all allocated traits are revealed, along with the plant food amount in the center.

Then in turn order, a player can either:
1) Take a plant food token for one of their herbivore/omnivore specie, modified only if the specie's trait(s) give it an advantage.
2) Attack another player's specie (or one's own) if your specie has a carnivore trait AND your body size is larger than the prey's size, subject to any modifiers given by the attacker's and prey's traits. If successful, take a number of meat food tokens from the general supply (which is separate from the plant supply) equal to the body size of the prey; the prey's population is reduced by one.

Players continue to feed their species in turn order, until everyone has fed up to their population level, or until the plant supply in the center runs out (much like satisfying the demand in Automobile), or until all carnivores are unable to feed. All species then drop in population to the level they were able to feed. All players then "bank" the food they had collected for each specie.

If population goes to zero due to attacks, that specie is extinct, with all associated traits going into the discard. However, the player gets a number of new cards equal to the discarded traits, so a player is not completely wiped out like in other evolution games.

The game ends when the card deck is exhausted. Players add up all banked food, population level of all species, and the total number of traits on surviving species. Whomever has the most points wins.

At first glance, this reminded our group of another Kickstarter game that was a terrible copy of Evo. The game seemed a bit too much "take that", especially with a bunch of carnivore traits showing up. The game play seemed too simplistic and not meaningful, since we had not totally appreciated the advantages of each trait. However, by the second game, it had become apparent that this game has a lot of meat to it, given the various interactions that the traits give--it takes a game or two to fully understand and appreciate almost all the available traits, because it takes actual play of the trait and seeing it in action to really "get it". The entire game feels like a biological microcosm, brutal and cold, yet elegant with an self-regulating intelligence. Herbivores struggle to survive amidst carnivores; too low on population and body size, and they will be hunted into extinction. Carnivores need to keep the herbivores in check, and keeping the number of species down, lest they get overwhelmed by sheer numbers and defensive traits. Grow too much population, and eventually the food supply will vanish, and the populations will adjust back down. Growing in size gives advantages in attack and defense, yet if attacked successfully by another carnivore, the larger body size gives more meat food tokens to the attacker. Stay small, and you may be attacked easier, but certain traits make it easier to defend. On the other hand, spawning new species is possible food for the carnivores, but more cards during the draw at the beginning of each turn--more cards drawn means more traits/population/body size to beef up your existing species. At some point, if this is left unchecked, then there is a serious leader runaway problem. However, with a 60 minute playtime, this is acceptable. Also, you have no one to blame but yourself and groupthink in allowing it to happen.

Then, there is the symbiotic traits between the various species of a single player. Depending on arrangement, a specie's trait may give defensive advantages to species to the left or the right, depending on the trait, such as "Warning Call" and "Symbiosis". It may also give feeding advantages, such as "Cooperative" or "Foraging". While certain traits make it harder to attack another specie, there are traits that negate them, or having a larger population and/or larger body size may overcome the trait. All in all, this is an interesting rocks-paper-scissors interaction among the various traits--no one trait is the best, and while advantageous one round, is obsolete by the next, since species are allowed to "morph" into a new set of traits. One day they are quiet foraging herbivores, while the next day they are pack-hunting, climbing carnivores! So, there is an very tense period where players watch each others discards and allocation of traits.

This is a very well made game. The ebb and flow of the rise and fall of species is fascinating to watch, and equally entreating as players frantically play cards to make their species survivable or to flourish, in response to the traits/population/body size of the other species, as well as the available food supply. There is plenty to think about here and enjoy. It is a solid "8".

RATING: 7.5
I enjoy this game, though prefer playing at 3-4.
Many variables to keep track of: population, body size, defense traits, actually eating [scoring points]. You can't just ignore the other players too, you need to see what their defenses are, how much food they are going to be taking away from the middle, if they can eat you, etc, which is why lower player counts are generally preferred. 

It may be a bit too much for most people, and if it is (sniff), you can try introducing them to Evolution: Beginning first to lure them in and then when they are ready for something more gamery/strategery, pull this out.
Though I have noticed my friends that liked to chomp on other players in E:Beginning did not like the jump to Evolution (where it is harder to chomp other players). But the players that were more pacifists liked the traits that enable better plant chomping.

RATING: 8
Theme: Nature
Gameplay: Play cards to evolve your species, taking advantage of the game "environment"
Components: Beautiful watercolors on the cards, heavy cardboard species boards.

RATING: 8
For a game with only a few options there is quite a bit of strategy.  When to attack, when to defend, when to go extinct... these decisions will have your head spinning.  Food = victory points so feeding first is a big benefit.  If you're going last, have a carnivore in reserve... just make sure you can feed it.  

Build your population early and add species if you can. Food will become scarce in the later game, so plan for it with a couple of well placed traits.

Played on BSW, but finally bought my own copy of the game.  The components actually make the game-play face-to-face a better experience.

RATING: 7
In Evolution, players use a fresh hand of cards each turn to create species, add traits to existing species, and grow their populations and body sizes. The key to the game is creating species that can favorably compete against the other players' species for a limited and unpredictable food supply. Consumed food, population sizes and active traits score at the end of the game.

You can assign up to three traits to any species and they generally fall into three categories - offensive, defensive, and increased consumption. They key is being able to suck down food faster than the other players since the supply is limited and food = points. The carnivore trait allows you to feed on other species rather than the food pool, but only if you have a larger body size. A lot of the negative comments I've read on this game cite the "take that" nature of the carnivores as a major turn off. While I agree that in the first play or two the game can feel that way, that's only because one hasn't yet grasped the symbiotic nature of the entire ecosystem. Sometimes the best response is not to play defensive, but to breed faster than you can be eaten, or create scavengers so that when you are fed upon it advantages your species as well. I don't see this game has having a strong "take that" element at all and those who think so I just don't think have given it a fair shake. You can also feel at a disadvantage in a first game not knowing the cards, but there aren't that many and it takes about half a game to get them down. This game has been a winner with all ages and all experiences this month, making it a good "go to" game to have in the collection!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
If there's such a thing as a well-designed take-that game, Evolution could be it. For me personally, it's an impossibility. Without a doubt, there are relevant decisions in Evolution. There are clever moves. It is possible to nurture a super predator or an invincible veggie eater. But the frustration of receiving the short end of the stick wipes out all enjoyment there could be. 

Up to a certain point, it's possible to shield one's animal against predators, to prepare it to gather enough food from the waterhole before others do, or to set up your predator for successful hunts. But in the end, one player having the right trait, the other not receiving what he needs, decides about survival. It's not about being faster than the predator, being faster than the prey next door suffices. Predators are not fair, players playing them even less. Once being an easy target, the vultures gather and demand their share of the slaughter.

RATING: 7.3
Family friendly game with more to it than meets the eye. In evolution you are trying to keep your species from becoming extinct. To do this you have to eat food, increase population and body size, but watch out for carnivores....they only eat meat and you are on the menu. The goal of the game is to have eaten the most food but the bigger your population and the more traits your species has also gain you victory points. Darwin had it right when he deduced that the most dominate species will always survive...and extinction isn't an option in this game!

RATING: 8
Do you like creating little creatures with scientific sounding names? Good, you chose the right game.  In this game you will decide which attributes and traits are given to each of your populations of creatures. Evolve one race to be able to outwit a carnivore! Or burrow into the ground! Or make your creature bigger than your predators, or even make your creatures hungry for flesh(carnivore).  The game is nice to setup and as long as you understand the game explanation is easy to new players, 15 min explanation max. The game is cute on the outside but does have some hidden complexity with how you can manage your species. The expansion "Flight" is also a nice touch that in hindsight was something lacking in the original game. Games last just shy of an hour, fun to play with 2 players and up. Good for casual game night, family night, and moderate game night. The twilight imperium players may get bored.

RATING: 7
Solid family game with an awesome theme. Grow your species and feed them. Players take turns playing as many cards from their hands as they want to either create a new species, increase their population/body size, or give their species' traits. One card must be used to contribute to the watering hole. After all players have used their cards everyone goes around taking food from the watering hole until everyone either eats or starves. Fun times. I'm going to hold off on buying this one and just get Evolution: Climate instead, due to the impending updates.

The game itself is not bad, however I feel the game goes at almost too fast of a pace and often can make performing tactics very difficult. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
One of my favorite games from Essen 2014. I just love the easy and yet intricate play! It absolutely feels like evolution.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
While not as awesome as Primordial Soup, another game with similar theme and feeling, it plays in half the time. Not to mention it plays decently enough with both three and five players, while Primordial needs exactly four to work.

Evolution is, at heart an economical game about optimization with an evolution theme, yet it has enough interaction, both direct and indirect, to avoid falling into the pure snowball economies trap. Speaking of interaction, don't expect it to be a conflict-heavy game. While carnivores do set other players back, a conflict-heavy carnivore won't score as many points as a herbivore, not to mention completely negating a carnivore is rather easy: players just need to commit resources to it. Actually, most of the damage carnivores do is indirect, forcing other players into spending resources in defending. Resources which would otherwise have been invested in scoring.

All in all, Evolution is a light-to-medium weight game with great replay value, as there are lots of possible tactics. While the rules are simple, and cards have multiple uses, which mitigate the luck factor tremendously, actually knowing how to deal with sub-optimal draws requires an in-depth understanding of the rules newbies lack. Thus, first plays can be frustrating.

Rating: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
Optimal number of players:

:thumbsdown:2,6

RATING: 10
[u]Awards:[/u]
[b]Game of the Con - Game Storm 2015[/b]
[b]Game of the Year - 2015[/b]

[u]Comments:[/u]
I didn't want to like Evolution, I really didn't. I don't think the artwork is very good, and the box is kind of hideous. I was at Game Storm 2015 and a friend came for the day and wanted to give it a shot so I checked it out of the library there. After setting up and reading through the rules I got really excited. The rules were easily understood and explained, so much so that I did it three times as people kept wanting to join in on the game so we would start over from the start. The components were well though out and very functional. Once I started playing the game I knew I had found a game that I had been searching for for awhile, an excellent evolution game. I have played a few hoping that they would be the holders of such a crown, but alas they never did. Evolution is by far and away the winner of that crown. Its mechanics while simple are pure genius in their effort to mimic what it is like for species to evolve over many million years. You start with one species and on your turn you can give that species up to three traits, or discard cards from your hand to make the species bigger or make its population grow. Once everyone has done this its feeding time. Herbivores eat from a common pool of food that everyone takes one token at a time for for any of their hungry herbivores, but this is also where the balance between carnivores and herbivores in an ecosystem really shines as a mechanic in the game. Instead of eating plants carnivores will eat other species at the table. If multiple carnivores are at the table you could see a cold war escalation as one tries to outdo the other so that they can be the sole apex predator in the system. At the end of the game you will score points for every species that doesn't go extinct (have their population drop to 0) as well as all the food your species ate throughout the game. Every time I play Evolution new strategies emerge and I find myself thinking about strategies in the game when I am not playing it. Overall the game is pure genius and needless to say as soon as I got home I ordered myself a copy. I can't wait for the expansion Kickstarter to kick off.

RATING: 8.5
Not only is this game mechanically solid, but it is also thematically solid. I feel like I am watching literal creatures grow and adapt themselves to an ever-changing environment. When there is food to spare, the plant eaters find ways to eat more of it. When food becomes scarce, species turn carnivorous and no one is safe. When carnivores stalk the land, species develop defensive techniques to prevent from being eaten. This will continue to get a lot of play at my table.

RATING: 7
Rating based on single 5-player game.
Lots of downtime, ultra chaotic, early setback could ruin the whole game leaves me very disappointed. Maybe next time I should try it with 4, or preferably 3 players and see if it would become better (with more control and less downtime).
Edited on 24/1/2015:
Played 2 more games with 4 players each, some have played the old version before. Some observations here:
1. All those players keep mentioning 2 things, I) This is the Evolution 'boardgame' while the old one is Evolution 'cardgame', and II) They won't play the 'cardgame' version again as this has become much more balanced now. I haven't played the old version so I can't comment it here...
2. It is very important to get the right moment to change from herbivore to carnivore, or vice versa. The carny to herby one is especially important, as you likely have invested some cards to up its size and put some talents on it.
3. With 4 players, the downtime is easily tolerated and feels more balanced. However luck is still quite high here: If you can't get the key cards during the required moments, e.g. climbing vs others, then you may be forced to consume your own species just to feed it. With a smaller no of players it would be even easier for a player getting some perfect combination while the other/ others can't get the required cards to tackle it/ slow it down. So I think 4 player game is the sweet spot for this game.
4. I am somewhat worried about the replayability. Although I rarely get to play a game more than 10 times, I could foresee that I should be able to explore all combinations soon enough. More importantly, MOST EXPANSION DESIGNS WON'T HELP IT BY SIMPLY ADDING TALENTS as it would only make being a carnivore more difficult to tackle the elusive herbivores. Right now the game is still quite welcome and thus the rating up from 6 to 7.
Edited on 19/9/2015: played a game with Flight expansion and with the 2nd edition cards. Thanks Power Wong for helping me to get a copy of the KS version!
The cards are more thematic and even more balanced in the 2nd edition cards. This could potentially be a game with rating 7.5 or even 8 if just with the 2nd edition cards. However, the Flight expansion is very ordinary and doesn't add much to an already very balanced game. Actually I suspect some combinations with flight cards could break the almost perfect balance in the basic game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
First impression based on one 3p game.  This game for me was an eye opener.  It taught me the difference between objective and subjective.  It taught me that just because a game is good it does not mean I will like it.  The components, graphics, production quality, rules and mechanics of this game are all above average to good.  The game even has a good degree of interaction in spite of being a "rummy" like tableau builder.  The theme is well represented in the mechanics, which simulate well some of the theories of the evolution of specialized traits and competition for scarce resources.  In spite of all of the above, I intensely disliked my first play of the game and do not wish to play it again.  It feels as though the game is mostly a "rummy like" MPS tableau builder with text based rule breaking/modifying cards, personal boards, and it feels as thought the decision space is limited and mostly driven by the random cards draw.  In other words, this is the kind of game where I feel the game plays me instead of me playing the game and where I feel disconnected and separate from the other players.  There are many other games that do this type of game better and this is also not the kind of experience I seek when playing boardgames with others.  Hence, objectively good game, subjectively undesirable.

RATING: 8.5
Needs more traits to really embrace the evolutionary theme and possibly action cards representing evolutionary concepts (like punctuated equilibrium, genetic bottle-necks) and events (famine, plague, sudden climatic change).
However, it is an absolute ton of fun to play.  Strategic and deep yet really easy to teach.  Does an exceptionally elegant job of marrying the evolutionary theme to the game play by something as easy as food availability.  Art is beautiful!
Biggest plus of all:  my wife really likes the game!
My search for a good evolution themed game is over.  This one hits all the right notes and will undoubtedly only get better with a couple expansions.  Love it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A really good thematic card game in which you attempt to devour food from a central watering hole and bite chunks off of each others species. Just don't get too attached to the creatures you "evolve" in front of you as you are going to be a KFC for someone.

I have really enjoyed the dynamics of this game. Each round you play out your hand of cards in turn order, either discarding cards to increase the body size of species or their numerosity, or you play them face down as a trait (adaptive evolution) reacting to the other creatures traits and the general environment the game has created. So the size of a creature and how many of them there are is open information as the turn proceeds, but the evolving adaptations are not. All traits are revealed at the same time each turn after everyone has played out the cards they want. Then like a scene from "From Dusk Till Dawn" the feeding begins. Teeth rip apart flesh, blood and scraps fly everywhere to be scavenged in the shadows, creatures hide behind others and co-operate to survive the best they can. Some gently sit munching grass getting fat, and then when they have had enough, gently burrow a tunnel and climb in it to avoid the carnage above. Each to their own.

In the game your creature needs to be larger than someone else in order to to be a successful meat eating dribbling sharp-toothed carnivore so if a player starts pimping out the body size of their burrowing, fatty tissued-up herbivore and then lay down a new unseen trait you have a suspicion straight away they have turned to the dark side and are going to be looking to chomp down on your grass-eating legs pretty soon. That is unless you started the bulking out race by sending your meek long-necked burrowing giraffe like mouse to the animal gym bumping up your body size and they claim they are getting bigger to stop you from eating them, but hey, you don't see things that way. No, No, No, they are the threat, you are peaceful, what harm is a long necked mouse going to cause anyone? or so you convince everyone else around the table, before revealing that long neck has a head that has now developed incisor teeth for ripping apart their flesh ... Whoops sorry didn't realise exactly what that carnivore trait meant! It's a dog eat evolved dog with not enough defences kind of world.

Luckily there are many ways of defending yourself and there is nothing more joyous as watching the changing expression on a players face as they proudly reveal their wonderful new carnivorous beast only to realise they can't climb and have no intelligence and all the other beasts of the plain have started climbing things, rapidly, and are now way up high completely out of their reach. 

Just a great game and definitely a keeper. Looking forward to the climate expansion, although how many unplayed expansions do you need? The base game is that good.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.6

Heavy enough to come back to again and again, but I think it'll be light enough to teach to others without much difficulty (not necessarily a gateway game, though; I'd say the rules put it more on par with Flash Point or 7 Wonders in terms of how much there is to teach).

We went through lots of playtesting iterations, and it seems like all the best decisions were made for the final version.  Exciting!

RATING: 7.5
FUN: amazingly thematic for a cardgame on such a broad subject. Very interactive without becoming too mean.

UNIQUE: a good game about evolution that plays easily and fast.

NEGATIVES: might be a little mean for some. New players might feel lost the first time as they do not fully appreciate all the possible interactions between cards.

Call me crazy, but to me this game almost feels like Small World: The Cardgame when carnivores are around. Instead of fighting for territory on a map, you are fighting more abstractly to let your species grow in order to get points. You are also looking for good combinations to gain an edge over the other players.

Számomra nagy meglepetés a magyar megjelenés, de ennek köszönhető, hogy beruháztam rá.
Gyönyörű a kivitelezés, és tematikus a játékmenet; tényleg küzdelem folyik az élelemért, és a fennmaradásért. Ellenben rendkívül egyszerűek a szabályok és a döntési helyzetek, ami nem baj, mert ezért gyorsan pörög a játék. Viszont könnyednek sem mondanám a kártyák tulajdonságai miatt, mert így elég sok mindenre kell figyelni, ami nehezítheti a játék elsajátítását. Számomra ez nem okozott gondot, ugyanakkor többféle kártya bőven elférne még, mert ez így hosszú távon nem elég változatos (nem szeretem ha egy játék csak kiegészítővel lesz újrajátszható). A több kártya miatt nehézségben eltolódna a komplexebb játékok felé, de ez nem baj (a Dicsőség Rómának! nagy kedvenc). Viszont így most se nem gémer játék, se nem családi.
De egy gyönyörű, egyedi témájú, érdekes társas, ami 6 fővel is játszható (és tök jól működik úgyis, ami hatalmas plusz!). Összességében egy hetesalá.

RATING: 8.5
Evolution ist ein wirklich tolles Spiel, mit einem faszinierenden und einzigartigen Thema. In Evolution entwickeln die Spieler eigene (urzeitlichen) Tierarten, mit unterschiedlichen und individuellen Eigenschaften. Diese werden durch karten dargestellt, die man zu Beginn seines Zuges zieht. Nun muss man sich entscheiden, ob man die Fähigkeiten der einzelnen Karten nutzt, oder diese abwirft, um sich eine neue Spezies zu erschaffen, oder die Werte einer schon bestehenden Spezies zu steigern. Schlussendlich geht es nämlich um den Konkurrenzkampf um die zur Verfügung stehende Nahrung. Aber Achtung, denn vielleicht hat sich ein spieler auch eine fleischfressende Spezies erschaffen, die es auf das Leben der anderen Tierarten abgesehen hat. Evolution ist immer wieder spannend und flott erklärt und ebenso unkompliziert gespielt. Klasse!

RATING: 7
Kinda cool how well the theme shows through in this simple and abstract game.  Basically you draw cards each round and either discard them to take actions (grow the size of your species/population, create another species) or play cards on a species to evolve them.  Everyone takes turns at the end of the round to feed by taking turns grabbing plant tokens at the watering hole - which can be scarce - or feeding on other species if your species evolved into a carnivore.  Lots of depth from an otherwise simple game.

RATING: 5
Rating as a game - 5.  Rating as a teaching tool - 9.  

Teaching Tool.  I've used this game as a teaching tool in an evolutionary psych class.  The purpose of the assignment is for students to become familiar with trait interactions, environmental stable systems / game theoretic thinking, and to consider how processes can be modeled.  The assignment begins with the students "critiquing" the game from the perspective of it being an accurate model of evolutionary processes.  The students are then required to play the game with limited sets of traits, while paying attention to "best" traits.  Finally, the students reskin the game using "psychological traits" and present their creations to the class.  Having done this assignment over two semesters, it seems to be something that the students respond well too, particularly the "re-skinning" aspect.  

Game. Now, I am referring here to the base game, and I have only played 2-player.  I have to say, I find the game unsatisfactory.  My main problem, I think, is that the game doesn't do anything well.  As a simple card game, I feel is lasts too long for what it is, and several of the traits don't have the specificity that I would expect in an intro / simple card game.  E.g., long neck and carnivore... is that simply and impossible combo?  What about forager and carnivore?  I also find that the game has a run away leader problem.  If a player gets a nice combo early (e.g., a cooperation chain mixed with forager and long neck), then that player is most likely going to win.  Sure, the length of the game helps mitigate this somewhat by giving an opponent time to attack that engine and build up one of their own... but this feels like a slog.  That is, a "Russian Front" of attrition.  I don't mind slogs or games of attrition, but they need some tension (e.g. Twilight Struggle or Star Wars Rebellion).  Here, the game just feels like a slog.  Finally, I feel like there is something of a disconnect between the various functions of the cards - discarding for feeding, discarding to increase body size, discarding to increase population.  That is, the discarding *decision* doesn't seem particularly stressful, in the way, say, that it is with Twilight Struggle.  Further, the thematic linkage of discarding cards (which are traits, after all) to speciate or trigger an increasing body size (which *should* be a trait) or to increase population (which should be a function of food acquisition), leaves me shaking my head.  All that said, I do find the game intriguing, but more because I want to like it more than I do.  I wish that the game specialized.  That is, I wish the game either became *more* of a party game (e.g., something like Machi Koro) OR specifically focused on some aspect of natural selection.  To me, the game *should* be about traits, in which case, the game should permit more complicated interactions...but then the game would no longer be family friendly, nor would I probably be able to use it as a teaching tool.

RATING: 7.5
Essen 2014
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
In this adaptation game, each player starts out with a species, with one population and one body size. You get dealt a hand of cards, and you can use the cards to increase population, increase body size, start a new species or give your existing species a trait, such as being a carnivore, getting more food, defence and so forth. Every turn, players secretly play a card out of their hand to seed the food in the middle, which can be large or small. Game ends when the deck runs out. 

It's a fairly simple game to play and understand. It's trying to predict what the other people will do in terms of turning into a carnivore that make it interesting. There are interesting choices to make and how to adapt your species. I am looking forward to more plays to get in a rhythm of how things change from round to round. 

In a game with 4 or more players, if one player turns out a carnivore, they potentially could put themselves in a kingmaking position. The carnivore doesn't generate enough points, but you can hurt a perceived leader. This would be my only criticism of the game, but otherwise I enjoy looking at the card combinations.

RATING: 8
A very good, solid Eurogame about creating a species with various traits and competing to see who can eat the most.  The species have access to trait cards, which allow them to add special modifiers (becoming carnivores, gaining extra food, defensive bonuses, etc.), gain a new species, grow your population, or increase in body size.  Population and body-size are marked with cubes and since there are so many cubes, you have to think carefully about how you want to evolve your species.  Do you want a lot of small creatures who are weak but can take a lot of food?  Or do you opt to be an apex predator who does whatever he wants?  (My species went unchecked last game with a large population, body size, and carnivorous traits.  I ran away with it.)  

The game plays quickly and takes about an hour.  I'd say it is a medium weight game with plenty of replay value.  If you like the thought of creating a thriving species, I'd consider checking this game out.

RATING: 4
I generally had fun for the first half of each game, but in each game, one superspecies would arise and dominate the whole game for what turned out to be a too long and forgone conclusion.

Since I have a pretty varied and changing game group, problems which always arise for new players are a barrier to enjoying the game for me, because I see them again and again.

That said, I DID have fun evolving my species and seeing the give and take of evolution when the game actually worked for my group.

Overall, for me, it is a bit too long for such a random game.

RATING: 6
[b]Evolution[/b]

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (10):[/COLOR]

[i]For me Evolution: The Beginning solves all the issues I have with Evolution. For starters Evolution: The Beginning plays great with 2; this was a goal of the designer when creating the new game. Carnivores are also viable out of the gate in Evolution: The Beginning, based on the game mechanics I'd be surprised if you didn't develop a few carnivores over the course of a game. Most importantly even though there are less trait cards in Evolution: The Beginning I don't see that as a drawback (I found the variety of traits in Evolution to be lacking) because the game plays so fast and there is plenty of tension in deciding how to get the most out of your limited resources (cards) as you race towards victory.

Long story short (too late...) I prefer the streamlined design of Evolution: The Beginning over Evolution and therefore I have very little desire to play Evolution again.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (6):[/COLOR]

[i]This was high on my want to play list, so I was happy when Drew offered to bring it to a meet up. My first play included the flight expansion, which in hindsight adds some much needed variety to the game play. After 3 plays I'm already finding the game to be severely limited in the amount of variety the evolution cards offer. 

In my opinion Evolution occupies the same game space as tableau builders like Race and Roll for the Galaxy, Elysium, Imperial Settlers and Deus yet is severely limited by a small set of species abilities. I understand that the combination of these abilities can change how the different species interact, but there are still only a handful of different ways to evolve your species. Also it is extremely hard to play a carnivore which my kids found really frustrating (even though they were eating me, it was only small bites so I was still raking in the points).. I was able to win 2 of the games I played while completely ignoring the carnivore strategy while my opponents were busy eating my dinosaurs, but unable to consume the same amount of food I was.

I think Evolution is a solid game and if asked I would play it again, but even with the Flight expansion this still isn't a game I want to own.[/i]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A much more evolved game than its predecessor. The game is drawing cards and then deciding which to discard for food, which has a power you won't find useful and maybe discard that to start a new animal, or build up the size of one of your existing animals, or do you really like the power and place it face up as a trait against one of your existing animals. Traits are either combo-y to get extra food as fast as possible, defensive, or offensive. There's little point building a nice combo to get food from the table if you ignore defence and get smacked about by some animal concentrating on offensive powers. A balance must be struck. Hopefully for the sake of the game, the offensive player(s) correctly targets the points leader - there's still an element of king-making in the game as a result, but much less than its predecessor. We enjoyed the game much more by deciding what our cards were going to do - boost size, population, trait, new species, etc - and then resolving them simultaneously rather than the potentially long-winded player-by-player process which could induce AP. With that approach, I didn't mind the combo-y nature of the game but I'm not sure how more there is to explore.

RATING: 3
Biggest disappointment in years. Bought into the hype, the look, the fun concept, and my wife loves animal games. She hated this. Half way through a much too long 2 player game, she says "this is boring. Was this made for five year olds?" 
I had to agree. Was this it? Eat and eat, very few choices, none of them interesting, too many random cards, repetitive. Lots of chrome, no game. Broken as a two player game. 40 card removal still not enough. Too easy to feed, most cards had high food values so food was always abundant, too easy to bring out more species, which soon filled the table, too hard to read all the various traits on all the animals to see if attack was possible or not. There were also many timing questions and card combo questions that implied 'strategy' and complexity but did nothing but detract from what I'd call 'Devolution', a very unevolved and uninvolving game. I make a carnivore, and she protects herself from it. Rinse and repeat. Games need to hook you immediately, not five games later, or they do not get played again. I don't even ask for ten plays. I want ONE fun game night, and I'm happy. If the game then sits in the shelf for two years, I don't care.  This game didn't even give us that. She asked "you paid $65 for this? That's absolutely ridiculous." I'm embarrassed, upset at hunting this down and going out of my way to buy it, and feel ripped off. 

We have never NOT finished a game. This was an unfortunate first. It's a $10 filler at best, way overpriced at $65 and would be better suited to young children. Considering I bought Capo Dei Capi for $10 last month, which provides so much more gameplay, and actually works for two players, it boggles the mind that this costs $65 CAD. More annoying is all this useless chrome takes precedence: huge Dino start piece, six tote bags, large watering hole. ????? It only jacks up the price and take up space.  This Dino has no clothes. It's a card game, and not even a good card game. 

Please, play online first and test before risking your hard earned money.

RATING: 6
Evolution is a card game in which you try to build up the traits of one or more species so that they can eat the most food.  Most animals are herbivores, and eat from a limited supply of food tokens that are distributed player by player until they run out.  However, some traits let species eat more than one food at a time, or get food from outside the supply.  Some traits can turn species into carnivores, which eat other player's species instead of food from the common supply.  There are lots of traits that provide extra defense against carnivores, and other traits that help the carnivores get around these defensive traits.  It was an interesting game, but as a card game it's very dependent on luck of the draw, and the take-that elements might turn some gamers off.

(2 plays)

RATING: 5
Alright, after a few plays with a range of 2-4 players, and ironing out the kinks each play, I can say that this game is solid, albeit maybe a bit light and repetitive for my tastes. The artwork is amazing and the component quality is okay. The boards are warping a bit and the card quality is something that needs to be sleeved ASAP.

My biggest complaint about the game is the watering hole and food tokens blend in with each other. Complete pain in the ass sometimes. I think the card play is fun and can lead to some interesting combos. I feel like there needs to be more cards to keep it interesting for more than a dozen plays.

Overall, I'm impressed with the game, but I could see my rating dropping a point depending on how the next several games go.

RATING: 8

RATING: 4
Survival of the fittest is fine for nature, but it's not good game design. Carnivores have incentive to attack the weakest species, which sets that player back, which makes them weaker, which makes them get attacked more, etc.

There is a major runaway leader (and spiraling loser) problem in this game; in all three of my plays, there have been one or two people clearly in the lead and one or two people with half as many points as the leader. If you get one or two good defensive cards in your opening hand, you'll be OK; otherwise, prepare for a bad experience.

RATING: 7
[i]I backed the Kickstarter for this, so if that biases my opinion, I apologize. [/i]

Evolution is a tableau-building card game, wherein each player is creating species in his tableau and granting them traits to offer protection, assist with feeding, or a number of other benefits. Meanwhile, other players are creating their own species. All of these will be competing for a limited common food supply. Well, until one of more of the species become carnivorous and then begin to eat one another. Points are scored by eating food, and are rewarded at the end of the game for living species and their traits.

The game is rather lighter than I thought it might be. There is certainly some strategy in setting up card combos to protect a species or eat food more efficiently, but it is very difficult to plan ahead as those choices are mostly determined from a rather small hand of cards each turn. There is a decent amount of player interaction through food competition and direct attacks on another player’s species. 

The game is well-produced (except for the cards - mine showed wear after a single play), and thematically interests me. For what it’s worth, my kids (9 and 7) both really took to it and have wanted to play repeatedly. It’s not something I’ll play all the time, but I do look forward to exploring it some more in the future.
