=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.3
P: 2-6, T: 60 min
Fairly simple, easy to teach but a wealth of elegant trait combos create fabulous depth. Mechanics enhance theme, which is educational, realistic and engrossing. Various successful strategies are viable, and yet deft tactical decisions must guide you to survive in a varied, chaning environment. Despite what some have been critical of, there is no one trumping strategy; each strategy has counter tactics if players are aware and a bit lucky with card draws to execute. Engine building card combo mechanics are balanced well against resource scarcity and need for survival; most engine building games end about the time you are really at max production (only growth is experienced), but most games of Evolution end after there has been some peak/crash with the available food. It's a great dynamic not found in many (any?) engine building games.

-The game is a bit more cutthroat than we usually like (but that also speaks to how much we like the gameplay and how it overcomes this drawback). Most games there is ebb and flow, or cat-and-mouse action that keep the experience engaging and fun. However, once in a while a player is able to really bully and dominate a game, which can leave a sour taste in everyone's mouth.
2 player experience not as satisfying getting to use 1 less trait and more lopsided results
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
In terms of games about evolution, I think this is my favorite one yet. It plays simply and has some nice interaction between players, in terms of the order at which you draft the food from the middle, and in terms of carnivores attacking each other. The combat system uses a sort of complex rock/paper/scissors system of determining legal targets which is just enough to be interesting without being too basic or too frustrating. Points are kept secret in this game, which means with more players it's harder to keep track of which players to focus on. In terms of complexity, some of the card interactions puts this slightly above gateway game, but quite a bit below brain burner.

:star: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:star: Handles 5 or more players
:nostar: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:nostar: Comfortable complexity
:nostar: Re-playability
:star::star::nostar: Enjoyment

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I like how the theme shows through the game. Basically it is card management, selecting cards to determine how much food to put in the common bank, start and grow grow species, or add traits to a species. Most species remain herbivores, but on trait makes it a carnivore. The other traits help species grow faster, get food earlier, defensive against carnivores, or a counter for the carnivores. 
Having a carnivore is challenging, you need you need traits and size to get around the other defenses. For the herbivores the limiting factor is food supply not enough your population starves and the effort you've put in lost. However, since the victory points are factor of the food consumed along the way writing off a species that has played out may not be so bad.
The card art work is nice and adds to the game.
It is a card game so it is dependent on the luck of the draw. Your plans have to be flexible depending on what traits you are drafting.

RATING: 8
Great game. Highly interactive. I love how these little systems fit together so well. Fantastic trait combos to explore. What I love is there is no guaranteed winning combo of traits. It all depends on what's happening in the simulated ecosystem created by the players.  What other players species trait combos are out, what they play and the amount of food available really forces you to adapt quickly. I love the colorful watercolor art.  The art in this game is very unique.  It's a shame the food tokens become camouflaged in the watering hole.  I've been using the back of the watering hole as a result. I wish the card stock was of better quality. Minor quibbles about probably the best game of 2014.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Theme – 4/5. Yay Evolution. Creating a species or group of species to survive forever. The Biology major in me doesn’t agree with parts of it, but generally it’s amazingly on theme and made to work.

Complexity – 4/5. Not hard to explain, the turns are laid out really well and you do the same thing every time. There aren’t a whole lot of cards, so it’s not really hard to catch on really quickly.  

Length – 3/5. Slight problem in that turns can either take a long ass time or are done in a heartbeat. Also the relatively short nature of the game (and it goes by pretty quickly) may feel a bit underwhelming when you have to pack it up. It doesn’t really feel like you accomplished a whole lot with your species when you wrap up.

Fun – 3/5. It’s a cool game, but you’re really just grab bagging stuff and going on auto-pilot. The only real decisions you have to make are less involving other people and more just the board state and optimizing what you are trying to get 

Replay-ability – 3/5. It’s cool, but the limited amount of cards that make its complexity low also make its replay-ability lower as well. You can only see pack tactics overcoming herding so many times before you kind of get bored with it.

Production Value – 4.5/5. Other than the small brown squares that you use to mark everything, the game is gorgeously rendered. I wish there was a bit more art, though I understand why there isn’t. The pieces are sturdy, not super easy to lose and just in general it’s a super well-made game that I expect to last me.

3.5/5. This is NOT a bad game. It is fun, interesting, and pretty well thought out. It just really is lacking a lot of substance in my opinion and while I ADORE the theme, I can’t see myself grabbing for this at every board game party, which is unfortunate because I really am quite a fan. I just feel like it really got to a point where it could have been extended, then they just stopped. Simplistic is good, but not in a game where you’re supposed to be making species interesting and individual.

Bonus: Customization – 2/5. Not a whole lot you can do except add bonus cards. It's possible and looks interesting, just would require extensive balancing.

RATING: 7
A simple fast game with interesting design. Players play cards to increase the size or population of their species or given them adaptive advantages. Then the various species feed, either from a central watering hole with a limited supply or by eating other (smaller) animals. Players score at the end fo the food their species have eaten and the size of the populations and extent of their adaption.

RATING: 5

RATING: 4
Pretty boring - there's a good bit of downtime, and I just didn't find much interesting in the game.  It feels quite a bit like Primordial Soup, but without any of the funny traits or spatial dynamics - I would always recommend PS over this game.  I like how in Primordial Soup you can still try to run away from a stronger, attacking player. And there seems to be at least one perfect set-up in the base game - Symbiosis-Cooperation-Long Neck on each species, with a increasing body size from left to right - that way you're impervious to all carnivores unless they have intelligence, and bid low on food pool since you're taking most of your food from the bank (6 per turn for 3 species).   Still in search for a game that really depicts evolution in any meaningful way - I really need to try Bios Megafauna one of these days.

RATING: 6
After another play of the game and some reflection, I think I have a good idea why I'm starting to dislike it more. It not only gives players the wrong idea of how evolution works (i.e. it somehow has a 'will' and/or 'strategy'), it sort of encourages, possibly sub/unconsciously, the idea of intelligent design.
I have a quite agnostic approach to life, but the fact of the matter is, if there is a theory that is proven to fit the evidence, up against another theory that has no evidence to prove it but has no evidence to prove its invalidity, the theory which has proof should be taken as canon. 

Its an interesting study to see the natural in-game development of trait-shifting, and i'm sure I'm missing something that educators see in this game. But overall, I'm quite wary about possible implied myths about evolution that many people already accepts as truth that the game gives. 
As a game-game though, its perfectly ok but there isn't much that grabs me to want to ever want to suggest it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
[BGCOLOR=#FFFF00][COLOR=#000000]Family.[/COLOR][/BGCOLOR] Wow, what a great game. Northstar hit it out of the park with a fully fledged leap from party games to Serious (tm) euro game.

The rules are simple (decide how to use your cards to grow your species, then make sure they can all eat each turn) but the combination of different abilities, how they interact with each other, and keeping an eye on how your competition is evolving, results in a Darwinian test of fitness on the road to victory.

We played it once and then immediately wanted to play it again to explore different strategies. The strategy isn't just limited to which abilities you choose, either; this is also a hand management game in which you have to make tough choices on how to spend your cards, when to add more species, or bide your time and build up a big hand, etc.

Love this game. I think the family is getting it for me for Christmas, woot!

RATING: 8.8
It needs a few playthroughs to puzzle out the valid strategies and how to counter them so no one is steamrolled, but once you do, you'll have a fine time. A very friendly game for those who like experimentation.

The game gets hard to track with 5+ players. 3-4 seems to be the sweet spot.

Hint: If you have bad luck with the cards, discarding for body size is a very, very good alternative.

RATING: 10
After teaching 25+ games, I can say this is the best tactical card game I have ever played. It is at once easy to PLAY, but the amount of DEPTH that emerges from the different combinations of traits and the unique environment created by the players at the table is mind-blowing. I find it so interesting that there is no best combination of cards to use, only the best-suited for the current environment, which changes each and every turn. Anticipating these changes and adapting your species to the ever-changing game state (and even influencing the game-state yourself) is extremely satisfying, one I don't expect to ever get old. 

I only wish it were easier to demonstrate the depth this game has to offer to new players. Inevitably, your first game is rough against experienced players because it is difficult to see the impact each decision has without experiencing the penalty, usually in the form of extinction (which can sting and leave a bad impression. Circle of life!)

RATING: 9
Missed the Kickstart on this game, but finally got my hands on it this week after it being sold out from the original distro.

This game was so much fun the first time we played that we immediately reset the game and played again.  We had so many laughs the second game, that my girl was late for work.

Great game, well designed, and all of the trait combos we put together seemed realistic to some creature that has or does exist.

We have only tried the game with 2 players so far, but have had 8 species playing at once.  I have no doubt the game will function just fine with more players and still be equally as fun.

The art work in the game is amazing.  Lots of vibrant colors and great drawings.

The rules are easy to understand, which makes the game play fun but not too elementary.

The construction of the board, trait cards, and species board are very sturdy and the game as a whole as well as the box seem like they will be very durable.

All in all we love this game, and we look forward to many more laughs.  Seeing my girlfriend's glee when her carnivore ate my species was worth every penny of the game.  lol  It's the little things that count during table top gaming I guess.

RATING: 7
2 plays

This is a cool little card game! You are dealt a bunch of cards each turn, which you can use to power actions like starting a new species, giving species traits, improving their size and population. Traits can be a variety of fun things like carnivore (eat other species!), camouflage (predators can't see you to attack), etc. Then, you go around taking food cubes one by one from a central pool (or eating other species). Populations decrease if you didn't collect enough food. All surviving species, though, score the food as VP. Play your way through the deck, then earn additional VP for your final species' populations and traits.

This is not at all like their usual party games (which are very good, of course). Instead, it's kind of a thinky, quick-playing card game. Quite fun!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a re-implementation of Evolution: The Origin of Species.  Players are each playing a species of animals and evolving them with cards that give them different traits while struggling to keep them all fed.  I didn't care for that game much at all but this one does a lot to fix the biggest problems of that game.

First of all, random dice rolls determining the amount of food in the watering hole has been replaced with players playing cards showing how much food they wish to place.  This is a great change as players not only have more control but it also forces them to acknowledge the opportunity cost of which card they cannot play as a trait each round.  Also, carnivores have been re-balanced.  I felt they were oppressively powerful in the original design and now have seen them both ineffective and extremely dangerous if played right.  I love the additions of population to keep a species out even after being eaten and body size as an inherent way to protect your species from carnivores, both also do a lot towards balancing carnivores from the original.  Finally, it is altogether just a lot cleaner.  You can still play each card in multiple ways.  As a trait, food, new species, or increase of population or body size but no longer have to deal with flipping the cards around and the increase of food cost has been replaced with a simple 3-trait limit on each species.  Also, the artwork is gorgeous and evocative.

The game really hits that sweet spot of theme and mechanics as well as simplicity and depth.  It's awesome to imagine what kind of creature you are creating with the provided traits and the game even comes with a grid to help you name your creature based on its triats.  It is also a game you can teach to almost anybody, the same deck of cards is used for pretty much everything and it all works really smoothly together.

All of this while providing a wealth of decisions and depth of mechanics.  I love the mechanic of having the same card provide multiple different effects.  It forces you to always consider the opportunity cost when you play the card for one purpose over another.  There is strategy to be found in every action.  Do you put more food in the watering hole or try to starve out other species.  Your strategy can even change considerably based on turn order.  First place can be important because you get the most number of shots at food and especially as a carnivore can eat another species before it can get to you.  While in last place when taking actions you get to alter your decisions based on what the players ahead of you do.

I do think it has a rich-get-richer problem.  It does its best to alleviate this by rewarding dead species with card draw of that species trait cards but still I feel like once you get ahead in this game it is hard to be unseated.

RATING: 7
The game takes on the evolution theme ala Dominant Species, but does it in a much more streamlined, thematic way. Your species truly evolve and can totally change (like from a vegetarian to a carnivore) to take advantage of the ecosystem. Unlike dominant species, you can teach the game in about 5 minutes and play your first game in an hour. It has that ideal balance of easy to learn, yet still strategic.

Ultimately it's a resource management game and has kind of a euro feel, but there is also direct player interaction. If you play the carnivore trait on one of your species, you can attack that species. But, you have to meet a lot of requirements like being larger in body size and you have to overcome any defensive traits they have. So a successful carnivore can easily feed while wiping out other species. But if they all build up defenses you can't adapt to, your species can go extinct in the blink of an eye. Also, the amount of plant food is variable and dictated by the players, so some turns there will be very little food available and only the strongest will survive. Definitely the best evolution themed game out there. I see no reason to ever play Dominant Species again. Rating might be a little deflated because I just played the game 6 times in the last 24 hours and I'm a little burned out on it.

RATING: 9
I'm a "the more complex the better" guy with board games, but this game stands in defiant exception to the rule. The game is simple, explaining it to rookies can be a hurdle but it's very straightforward once it clicks. All complexity is in card interaction, clearly printed in full (no glossaries needed for symbols or keywords). And yet something about the game just makes it wildly satisfying. For added fun, define your critters... Their journey is so much more fun when you qualify that your Mass 2 high pop Fat tissue defense herd critters were Guinea pig swarms, who over time grew massive and developed symbiotic relationship with your burrowing storks before suddenly eating them as they became carnivores. My only warning is that the game is VERY different with just two players, so make it a priority when you have groups. Both Flight and Climate expansions Are fun, but not must haves.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Evolution is a card game all about feeding your species. There's a limited pool of food resources (at least in theory, if not in our game) and competition to eat as much as you can. The winner is the player with the most: consumed food, population and evolved traits.

You start with a species and every round you draw 3 cards plus an extra card for every species you control. Every player plays a card to the center, which will determine how much plant food will be available in that round. Players play cards to evolve their species, start new species, or grow their existing species in either body size or population. Then in player order, players feed a species with whatever food they can find.

A species can eat food equal to their population, but if they fall short their population will starve down to whatever level was fed. Many of the traits are related to getting food. But there's also traits that let you become a carnivore, which allows you to feed off other players. If a carnivore is larger than you, they can eat one of your population, which gives them food equal to your body size. And there's a lot of traits that allow you to defend yourself against predators, but also cards that let them ignore your defenses.

I didn't play this game particularly well, and completely missed that body size was as important as it was. So I wasn't prepared at all when carnivores suddenly appeared. About halfway through the game I realized that I was just barely eking by and that the game was over for me. There wasn't much I could do at that point but wait for the game to finish.

It was pretty frustrating that we were playing a game with a lot of food on the board, and that I never saw the cards that worked well with a lot of food (foraging, fertile). I did finally get a foraging card, but was only able to use it once, and I never saw a fertile card at all. I mostly saw warning call, ambush, intelligence, carnivore, and hard shell. Once or twice I saw other cards like foraging or unstable DNA, but it didn't really make sense to play them when I got them. So on top of not playing well, the game hates me too.

I kind of want to like this game, but not getting to see many different cards left a really bad first impression.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
About [thing=155703][/thing]:

[b]1) What is it?[/b]
[thing=155703][/thing] is an strategic game that has hand management as main mechanic. It puts players in charge of creating and evolving species in order to feed and survive better.

[thing=155703][/thing] has a high amount of indirect and direct interaction. The indirect comes from every Herbivore mostly fighting for the same, and limited, amount of available food. The direct is attacking the species of other with Carnivores, which feeds them and lower the population of the attacked species.

There are several paths to follow and combos to be made, both inside the same species as well as with others. Decisions are, normally, not hard to be made, but are important, driving your options: your ability to eat, to resist attacks, to attack, etc.

Replay value is high: the random order and distribution of cards guarantee that the plays will follow different directions, not only in species created, but also in the availability of food, the interactions and timming.

The production value is good: the drawbags and player boards are neat, the tokens, board and cards are OK and it comes with an awesome first player token.

[b]2) How do you play?[/b]
Each player starts with a specie with 1 Population and 1 Body, and 4 cards. At the start of each round the player chooses one card from her hand to add to the pool of food that will be available (cards have numbers that says how mch food it will add - some have negative numbers). Cards can be used to:
- Add traits to a specie;
- Increase Population of a specie;
- Increase the Body of a specie;
- Start a new specie (which will begin without traits, and with 1 Population and 1 Body).

Cards can be kept in hand for later rounds. Once everyone has used the cards they want in the way they want, comes the Feeding phase. The cards added at the start of the round will be revealed and the total amount of food will be tallied. Then, starting with the first player, food can be gathered (for Herbivores) or hunted (for Carnivores). Herbivores take food from the pool of food created by the cards, while Carnivores hunt other species and its food comes from the bank (don't affect the central pool directly, but, by lowering the population of other species, it has an indirect effect).

Both Herbivores as well as Carnivores can normally only eat until the food amount reaches the number of Population of the specie. Carnivores can only attack species that have a smaller Body, even if this means attacking a specie owned by the same player that has the Carnivore. Only one specie of the player can attack or take food from the pool, then the feeding turn passes to the next player.

There are, as expected, traits that help gather more food (from the bank, like Long Neck, more from from the pool, like Foraging, etc), others that help protect from Carnivores, others to enchance the ability to hunt, and so on.

Food from the pool that isn't eaten stays for the next Feeding phase. When every has finish eating or there isn't more available food (be to take or to hunt), feeding is over. If a specie wasn't able to feed enough to reach their Population size, it loses population until the amount of food it was able to get.

Players take the food gathered and put them in the bag - they will count as points, at the end of the game.

The round is over. In the new round players will receive 4 cards plus 1 for each specie above 1 the player controls. Play phase continues the same, until the draw deck is empty, then the last round will begin. Once it is over, players will count all the food they gathered - each will be worth 1 point. Also, players will gain 1 point for each trait on the species they have, plus 1 point for each point of Population on the species they have.

The player with the most points will be the winner!

[b]3) Which are the decisions made during play?[/b]
The main one: how to use the cards you are dealt. This is valid from the start of the round (which card to add to the food pool) to the play phase - not only to select which Traits will work better with your specie, considering what the others have and what you want, but also to spend cards to increase Population (to gather more food: meaning more points), Body (to defend against Carnivores) and/or to start a new specie (if kept alive, will earn you more cards and increase the cap of the amount of food/points you can get per round).

These use of cards doesn't tend to be much difficulty - there are many you don't want (making it easier to discard) others that maybe are too important, and will be used as traits as soon as you see them. Yet, considering that the traits stay on the table and the increase in Population and Body are permanent (Population can decrease due to hunger or by being hunted), the choices carry weight for later rounds.

The other decisions that can happen often is which specie to feed first and which to attack if you have a Carnivore. Is possible that not all species will be able to feed, and extinction can, indeed, happen, not only due to hunger but also due to attacks of Carnivores, meaning that being able to keep a health amount of Population is very important, not only to being able to take more food (points), but to keep the specie around. On the other hand, this is why deciding which specie to attack is important, both to hurt someone that is doing too well and as a bargain chip if there are other Carnivores around.

Overall, decisions matter and have consequences, but aren't overly complicated nor too difficult.

[b]4) What are the good things in the game?[/b]
- Production value: the art if neat, the components are nice, everything looks great on the table, specially the first player token;
- High replay value;
- Theme has a strong presence;
- Offers constant decision making;
- Different paths to follow to victory;
- High amount of interaction, both indirect and direct.

[b]5) Which are the bad news?[/b]
- Luck has a strong presence, due to the random draw of cards, normally something unwanted in a strategy game;
- Has strategy, but tatical decisions matter more;
- Being able to attack with Carnivores can both lead to some bashing the leader and, worst, to attack players already behind, inducing to a vicious cycle;
- Some cards seems much better than others.

[b]6) How do you feel while playing?[/b]
In a very thematic enviroment: the game does create a microcosmos of Nature, in which food is coveted, hunters pray on the weak (and meaty), and evolution makes species better in surviving and grow - be to take more and/or being able to protect themselves, directly or with the help of others. This is truly an achievement, specially for a card game with a few tokens and cubes.

I was excited to play [thing=155703][/thing], as it seemed to fit my bill almost perfectly: theme, reasonable playing time, options and decisions mixed in a not too heavy form. But my first play was lackluster: I was attacked before being able to make my game really start, and this, coupled with some bad decisions, meant I felt like I was just rolling with the punches, not truly playing, instead I was just there - some more attacks meant I was near last when the game finished - the position wasn't an issue, if I at least felt I gave a good try. I didn't.

Then I played again and the experience was better: less players (from 5 to 3) in a more friendly enviroment (as things went, I had the only Carnivore for most of the game), I could develop my tableau, seeing and using more options provided by the game. I still lost, however, I did played.

Games that allow direct attack can have these issues: read the game state is important, but, sometimes, due to protections, is possible to be attacked even when the person doesn't want to do this against you: is simply the biological imperative survival. You better grow a hide and horns, or you won't be able to handle [thing=155703][/thing]: this is my warning call.

In the end, [thing=155703][/thing] gives a nice thematic experience, a good set of decisions and a high level of interaction, all put in a pretty package and a almost short playing time. [thing=155703][/thing] won't work for everyone, mostly due the random aspects and attacks, but can be a good option for those looking for a medium weight tatical game.

RATING: 5
The theme is great, and the cardplay is quite interesting, with an amazing amount of combinations from just a bare handful of cards.  There's some runaway leader issues, especially in the two-player game, though that's mitigated by short playtime.

The components here are great, with the puzzling exception of the cards.  Though they're the most important component, they're cheap feeling, difficult to shuffle, and prone to chipping on the edges with black borders.  Perhaps the lack of coating is to reduce the deck size?  But the rest of the components are great.  The art is wonderful, the starting player dino is incredibly cute, and the things like the trackers and the cardboard are heavy-duty.  There's even separate custom-printed drawstring bags for the players.  How gorgeous!

In the end, though, tthis game is confounding.  I have yet to actually have fun playing this game.  It's a weird mix of confrontational and light, in a way that is somewhat unsatisfying.  Every game has ended up feeling rather anticlimactic and just not fun.  It should be fun, it's just that I and the rest of my group just can't seem to find it.

RATING: 7
The game is very fun. However luck-driven, you have to pay attention to what your opponents play, and tactical choises are needed. If you want to win you basically have to do the same things, and have to use the same attributes again and again, but because card drawing determins your options every game will be a bit different, but not too much. Do not expect very deep strategy and deep development of species. It is more about changing attributes of species over the game to make them better or accomodate to the current status (no carnivorous for instance, or more carnivorous...). So the game is a light game with fun.

RATING: 9
A solid game.

To start, the artwork on the cards is absolutely stunning.  Not that this is necessary, but it ads to the beauty of the game and, when you spend hours playing, it helps to have something this nice to look at.

The game is simple enough to teach quickly, which allows people to get into the game with ease and limits the number of times you have to pull out the rule book mid game.

As for the actual gameplay, some of it is luck of the draw, as you have a deck of cards from which do draw.  However, what you do with these cards can have huge impacts and provide new combinations throughout the game.  With the so many traits and ways in which the traits can interact there is no one strategy which will always result in winning.

And since the cards you are dealt give you a random combination of traits (depending on how well you shuffled from the last game), you may easily find that the trait you thought was useless before has now become incredibly useful.  It took me almost a dozen games before I figured out how to effectively use fat tissue, but now it has a place in one of my strategies.

This game has the potential to make you think more strategically than chess, allows you to make changes to strategy that can seriously swing things, and you can be as confrontational or passive aggressive as you want.  It also lets you play mind games with people as they try to guess whether you replacing certain traits is a bluff or if you're completely altering your strategy mid game.

I tend to purchase games based upon a few things.
1) Do I think I'll like this game. (I'm spending the $)
2) Are there people in my gaming group that will like this game (I'll need someone with which to play)
3) Is the price worth the potential for play

When seeing it on Kickstarter I figured I'd like it and I might have one person in my gaming group that'd also enjoy it.  After playing I've discovered that I love it and there are many people in my gaming group that also really like it.  As for the price, that's up to each person individually.  For me, it was definitely worth the money, plus I was able to help something I believed in become a reality.

For me, the only thing keeping it from being a 10 is that there's always room for improvement with games and it's not quite universal enough  (though it's impossible for a game to appeal to everyone).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Evolution is one of my favorite games. It packs so much game into such a small card set and simple rules (including an amazing integration of theme and game mechanisms, a personal favorite of mine) that I find each game extremely satisfying, win or lose.

The multiple races within the game provide consistently fresh decision sets- the race for food in the watering hole, the race to build a plant-eating engine or defend against carnivores. Particularly pleasant is the balance the game presents between offering interesting tactical decisions each turn (what should I use this card for- it's ability or to create a new species, etc.) and having a moderate ability to play strategically as well.

Finally I find the different and interwoven levels of player interaction to be excellent. Directly competing with your opponents via carnivore attacks/defense or working to starve them out of their amazing engine through watering hole depletion are each just wonderfully fun to pursue.

The one con that I have found for the game is that it seems quite simple to players at first and so can be easily dismissed.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Evolution is a tough game to talk about because our first play of it was so soured by what turned out to be a botched rule. However, that excites me because I love the idea of the game: you have your species and you help them evolve over time while starting new species and helping them as well. You can be an herbivore and mind your own business or you can become a carnivore and start eating everyone else.

The carnivores are were we started going wrong in our game. Each species consists of a body size, a population, and up to three traits. Whether you’re an herbivore or carnivore, you need to eat food up to your population size: you have four population, you need four food. However, an herbivore eats plant food from the watering hole and a carnivore eats the other species. When they eat another species, that species loses a population and the carnivore gains food from the food bank (not watering hole) equal to the attacked species body size. That’s what we got wrong, instead we just assumed one attack equaled one food and that led to a lot of species getting needlessly wiped out. In addition, it led to some interesting attempts at preventing the carnivore from winning the game by maxing out body size to make it harder for the carnivore, but in that case the carnivore would only need to eat once.

I’m withholding judgment on this game, but I look forward to trying it out again with the correct rules.

RATING: 10
Best boardgame I've played, highly highly recommend Evolution as it's a really legit great game, and best of all it's a troll game but it's legit trolling.

Evolution is essentially a game where you play god as you try to create the ultimate creative to survive life and predators from other players, by growing your species, giving them traits(insurance) to protect themselves or get more food which are your points. Once the card deck runs out and you play one more pile of it, the game ends and you total up your points to see who has the most food along with other factors that give you points.

I love this game, this game is so fun that I almost can't really think of any flaws for it, it's that fun.
So what's the con I can think of for this game? Well just certain specifics on how some traits work aka the rules for them, but it's pretty minor and you'll either figure it out or come to an agreement on it.

I don't know why any thinks 4 players is the best to play with because I think it plays best from 3-6 players. 2 is possible but certain parts of the rules are changed to accommodate 2 players, and that's fine but the more the merrier.

RATING: 5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I learned a dozen new games this past weekend at a local con, and surprisingly this was my favorite.

[i]Evolution[/i] is interactive, well-designed, easy to learn (without being too simplistic), and seemingly a decent simulation of the rise and fall of imaginary species.

My only concern is whether certain card combinations are so powerful as to be insidious game-killers, and whether it's ten times more fun to be a carnivore than a herbivore.  Either issue could be a drag on an otherwise excellent game, though the latter concern is largely mitigated by the fact that players can control multiple species simultaneously.

I'm hopeful that we won't hit the "we've figured this out" ceiling anytime soon, and at first glance the game is good enough that I could be coaxed out of my purchasing hibernation to acquire a copy.

[i]Update:[/i]

Peaceful players will discover that fielding a herbivore-only team isn't terribly exciting.  And if no one goes carnivore at all, the game falls into a rut of fighting over scraps of plant food.  But it isn't always fun to be a predator, either, since many improvements are anti-carnivore - which can lead to quick extinction for new meat-eaters.

Looking to buy? Since I'm all about truth in advertising, there are five reasons why you might hate this game:

1. You don't like being "mean" to other players by eating their species. (Your own carnivores could eat your own smaller species, but what fun is that?)

2. It makes you upset when your species are eaten by other players' carnivores.

3. If no one decides to become a carnivore (due to #1 or #2), this literally becomes the most boring game of all time. As described above, many traits are anti-carnivore - and therefore useless in an all-herbivore game - and herbivore players are limited to low-key food competition and spawning new species.

4. Some trait combinations are quite powerful, and certain players can get lucky and draw those particular traits.

5. Due to the multi-layered trait interactions, there is a potential for significant downtime, depending on the number of players and their thinkiness. I'm not sure that I'd play with more than 4 players, for instance, since keeping track of the trait combinations and body sizes would cause me to blow a processor.

Anyway, I can see this being a great game with my high-interaction gaming group. People will enjoy eating one another, watching species starve for lack of food, laughing at the victims, and getting revenge. If that isn't your bag, you might skip it.

It's certainly worth a play, either way. It is a very good game, and even better with the [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/boardgameexpansion/176173/evolution-flight]Flight[/url] expansion.

I played this once with 6 players and we were all new to the game.  I really like the mechanics for evolving and growing your species of creatures and playing to get the most food at feeding time was interesting.  I don't feel like I really got to see the whole of the game though as a lot of high cards kept getting played for food and there were very few carnivores in our eco-system so very little dying seemed to be happening which was a little concerning.  I could see my rating going up if the game shins more with experience... or down a touch to a solid 6 if its just a light, quick card combo and score game.  I'd certainly like to play again to find out!

RATING: 7
Egy kétfős játék után lemondtam az előrendelésem. Egyáltalán nem rossz, de ennyi embernél egész egyszerűen vmi hiányzik belőle. Többen biztos jobb, nem akarom lehúzni, adok még neki majd esélyt, de ez nálam így nem must have, főleg nem ennyiért. A pontszám is csak iránymutató, mehet ez feljebb is, ha többen játszotok.
---
Kipróbáltuk négyen is, de nem változott a véleményem. Egy erős 6 pont, ennyi, DE
adok neki még egy esélyt, egyszerűen olyan jók a kritikák róla, h esélyes, h vmit én nézek be vele kapcsolatban. Szeretem a tablóépítős játékokat, jó a téma is, így egyelőre nem engedem el a dolgot.
---
A 4. játék meggyőzött, h én néztem be vmit, mert összességében ez egy jó és ötletes kártyajáték. Nem mondom, h kötelező, de mindenképp próbára érdemes a tablóépítés szerelmeseinek. Lehet konfrontatívan játszani, lehet békésen legelgetni, de a kombinálás és a szituációkra reagálás fontos elem, ez adja a játék sava-borsát.

RATING: 5
This is a pretty average game to me. What I like about it is the interplay between people that really looks like it simulates evolution, as well as the fast game time and ease of learning. I play this with larger groups of people (3+) as 2-player seems a little gimped and boring. The game is very easy to learn and we play this enough with some of our less board game inclined people. The mechanics are overall average to me however and I like most of my other games a lot more.
